<div id="review_627788531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/627788531" />
    <a title="Frank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2458054-frank"><img alt="Frank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572407891p2/2458054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/627788531?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank" href="/user/show/2458054-frank">Frank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780679641759" href="/book/show/8098731-the-lives-of-the-noble-grecians-and-romans-2-vols">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458054-frank?shelf=history-politics">history-politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458054-frank?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458054-frank?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2458054-frank?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer627788531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2739265342808303366">Finally, I’ve made it through Plutarch’s Parallel Lives. The text itself is over seventeen hundred pages and consists of biographies of notable Greeks and Romans. Artaxerxes II is thrown in near then end to give opportunity for oriental bashing. <br/><br/>The biographies are a series of character studies illuminated by telling incidents and anecdotes of the type,: “.. and then one of Gaius Julius Caesar’s rivals challenged him by remarking X, and quick-witted Caesar turned the tables, responding Y.” This </span>
  <span id="freeText2739265342808303366" style="display:none">Finally, I’ve made it through Plutarch’s Parallel Lives. The text itself is over seventeen hundred pages and consists of biographies of notable Greeks and Romans. Artaxerxes II is thrown in near then end to give opportunity for oriental bashing. <br /><br />The biographies are a series of character studies illuminated by telling incidents and anecdotes of the type,: “.. and then one of Gaius Julius Caesar’s rivals challenged him by remarking X, and quick-witted Caesar turned the tables, responding Y.” This reminds me of some of the encounters between Jesus and the Pharisees. However, the anecdotes make no sense unless you are already quite familiar with the actors– 50 protagonists and many times more supporting characters - who Plutarch writes about. I’d encountered just about all previously, for instance in Herodotus, Thucydides, or in Mike Duncan’s excellent History of Rome podcast. Yet I still found frequent need to remind myself of the background. Together with background reading, climbing Mount Plutarch amounted to something like 4000 pages. <br /><br />Did I like Parallel Lives? There are big pluses and minuses. In places, the writing is as beautiful as anything in Shakespeare or Goethe. During my years in England I had often pondered why it was the Brits tolerated astonishing levels of technological and economic ignorance among senior civil servants and administration officials, while simultaneously insisting on firm foundations in Greek and Roman classics. With Plutarch, I begin to anticipate the answer: among its many lives we encounter rulers of every disposition facing every imaginable situation; excellent training for any leader. Plutarch’s scheme of relating narratives of two similar or contrasting personalities, then drawing comparisons is both entertaining and pedagogically effective. After a short while I found myself reciting my own comparisons: The Lives of Cantor and Gödel; Hunter Thompson and Hemmingway; John Cleese and Steven Fry 😊 .. The technique is so effective I wonder why it has been used so rarely. <br /><br />Then there are the things in Plutarch I didn’t like at all. The overwhelming majority of his portraits are of military leaders, and we are led through a never-ending recital of battles, rebellions, and victories. I was reminded why I hated history during my school days. The few cases where Plutarch deviates form the military formula, he takes up senior political leaders/rhetoricians (often to throw an alternative light on the same characters). These are more interesting. But still, at times I had to think of those books sold at airport waiting rooms, promising to teach young managers the seven steps to becoming a great leader. Yech! Frequently, I asked myself: where is the comparison between Chrysippus and Seneca, between Aristophanes and Juvenal? Too late, I reaslised I might have used my time to read Diogenes Laertius.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2739265342808303366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_536510323" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/536510323" />
    <a title="Darran Mclaughlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5897674-darran-mclaughlin"><img alt="Darran Mclaughlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316944864p2/5897674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/536510323?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darran Mclaughlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darran Mclaughlin" href="/user/show/5897674-darran-mclaughlin">Darran Mclaughlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486445762" href="/book/show/279534.Greek_and_Roman_Lives">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5897674-darran-mclaughlin?shelf=greek">greek</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5897674-darran-mclaughlin?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5897674-darran-mclaughlin?shelf=roman">roman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer536510323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1989646826060125734">A fascinating book. The gulf between these famous Greeks and Romans and we today is huge. It is interesting to see the similarities and differences between their day and ours. Their attitude towards death is certainly hugely different to ours, as they seemed to be ready to risk their lives far more willingly then we are. Another major difference involves what was expected of a man. Today people are often more specialized and tend to conform to some subculture or other, but with the ancients a ma</span>
  <span id="freeText1989646826060125734" style="display:none">A fascinating book. The gulf between these famous Greeks and Romans and we today is huge. It is interesting to see the similarities and differences between their day and ours. Their attitude towards death is certainly hugely different to ours, as they seemed to be ready to risk their lives far more willingly then we are. Another major difference involves what was expected of a man. Today people are often more specialized and tend to conform to some subculture or other, but with the ancients a man was expected to balance a range of accomplishments, so you have the example of the most popular, attractive aristocrat in Athens choosing to spend most his time with the poor and unattractive Socrates instead of carousing with other young aristocrats. You were expected to develop your mind through study, practice and appreciate philosophy, poetry and music, be graceful and attractive in your looks and manner, engage in athletic competition, be physically courageous and willing to go to war etc...etc... I suppose this is an aristocratic conception of life that has fallen away but why have we discarded the idea of this kind of balanced development? <br /><br />The edition I read contains the lives of Solon, Pericles, Alcibiades, Themistocles and Alexander of the Greeks, and Marius, Sylla, Pompey, Caesar and Cicero of the Romans. I generally found the Greek lives more interesting then the Romans but they were all very interesting. There was a greater sense of courage, cunning and individual heroics about the Greeks. I suppose all the Romans performed their deeds with the backing of the Roman Legions whilst the Greeks didn't have a massive standing army to rely upon, and what they achieve often involved having to persuade lots of free thinking individuals to back them. <br /><br />The Lives are the source of many well known quotes and anecdotes. Can you imagine a modern equivalent to Diogenes being visited by Alexander the Great and upon being asked what the most powerful man in the known world could do for him replying that he could step out his way and stop blocking the sunlight? </span>
  <a data-text-id="1989646826060125734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer459116211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6004834411974347957">This is often known as the "Parallel Lives" because these biographical sketches come in pairs, one Greek, one Roman, followed by a comparison. This is a thick tome. My edition of Plutarch's <i>Lives</i> as translated by Dryden is nearly 800 pages. And yes, I read the whole thing and was never bored. Maybe this makes me perverse, given the number of reviewers I've heard describe them as dry. I thought it a wonderful and engaging introduction to the most illustrious personalities of Greco-Roman antiquity</span>
  <span id="freeText6004834411974347957" style="display:none">This is often known as the "Parallel Lives" because these biographical sketches come in pairs, one Greek, one Roman, followed by a comparison. This is a thick tome. My edition of Plutarch's <i>Lives</i> as translated by Dryden is nearly 800 pages. And yes, I read the whole thing and was never bored. Maybe this makes me perverse, given the number of reviewers I've heard describe them as dry. I thought it a wonderful and engaging introduction to the most illustrious personalities of Greco-Roman antiquity. I first read these when I was a college dropout for a time, and was reading through <i>Good Reading's</i> "100 Significant" books so my brain wouldn't turn to mush: I found it a favorite. Maybe it helped that by then I had made my way through Homer, Aesop, the four surviving Greek playwrights, Thucydides, Plato, Aristotle, Lucretius, Vergil. Given that from the time I was a teen I was a fan of Mary Renault's and Robert Graves novels about ancient Greece and Rome, and familiarity with Shakespeare's plays (several of which were based on Plutarch) that means quite a few of the figures featured were already familiar to me: Theseus, Pericles, Alcibiades, Coriolanus, Cato, Crassus, Pompey, Alexander the Great, Julius Caesar, Cicero, Brutus. Maybe that helped. But there were also a lot of figures then unfamiliar to me such as Sulla and Lysander and the book never lost my interest. <br /><br />From what I gather it's not always reliable as history. Plutarch purportedly stretched things, both to find similarities in the two figures paired and to draw a tidy moral. And given Plutarch was a Greek <i>and</i> a Roman citizen trying to underline what they had in common, as you could expect, those outside that charmed circle, such as Cleopatra (for all she was of Greek descent) and the Carthaginians don't exactly get good press here. It probably is a good idea to seek out an edition that's thoroughly annotated--and try different translations if you don't find Dryden congenial. But I for one think this is numbered among the great books for good reason.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6004834411974347957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2162698565" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2162698565" />
    <a title="Dennis Murphy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58910608-dennis-murphy"><img alt="Dennis Murphy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471961577p2/58910608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2162698565?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dennis Murphy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dennis Murphy" href="/user/show/58910608-dennis-murphy">Dennis Murphy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58910608-dennis-murphy?shelf=for-all-mankind">for-all-mankind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58910608-dennis-murphy?shelf=recommended-books">recommended-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58910608-dennis-murphy?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58910608-dennis-murphy?shelf=on-war">on-war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2162698565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6252830079335454125">Parallel Lives of Noble Greeks and Romans by Plutarch is probably one of the most profoundly human narratives I had the pleasure of reading. The book is a veritable library of different biographies of key figures from the ancient past. What we have left is sadly incomplete, with some venturing to suggest that the current corpus is less than a third of the total venture. <br/><br/>The basic concept behind the book is to share the story of great mens' lives, but as a testimony to their character and their f</span>
  <span id="freeText6252830079335454125" style="display:none">Parallel Lives of Noble Greeks and Romans by Plutarch is probably one of the most profoundly human narratives I had the pleasure of reading. The book is a veritable library of different biographies of key figures from the ancient past. What we have left is sadly incomplete, with some venturing to suggest that the current corpus is less than a third of the total venture. <br /><br />The basic concept behind the book is to share the story of great mens' lives, but as a testimony to their character and their fortune, rather than a broad historical narrative. Many major scenes are go unmentioned simply out of the assumption that any good Roman of the Antique world would already be well familiar of the subject, and could easily obtain wanted information. Now, much of what these biographies are based off of is lost, making his brief windows sometimes the only information we have. <br /><br />While people often look to the Alexander and Julius Caesar biographies, these were less interesting to me. The last of biographies on Sparta: Agis and Cleomenes, made up the most poignant of the last of the Greeks, while both Crassus and Pompeii come across as strangely ennobled by Plutarch's pen. Cato the Younger and Brutus earned my ire nearly as much as they earned by respect. Then there were those that I knew little of, such as Camillus, Coriolanus, Timoleon, Amelius Paulus, Sertorius, Agesilaus, and Dion were introduced to me from a position of near total ignorance. <br /><br />This book has turned into an art-form the idea of history and human identity as a single enterprise. Though I understand that this book is long (well over a thousand pages, regardless of the version), archaic (most of the translations are old, and even the newer ones are translating a book from almost 2000 years ago), and far removed from the day-to-day buzz, there is perhaps no author from the antique world I recommend more highly. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6252830079335454125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_114216150" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114216150" />
    <a title="Jorge Andrade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3278181-jorge-andrade"><img alt="Jorge Andrade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1265911255p2/3278181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114216150?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jorge Andrade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jorge Andrade" href="/user/show/3278181-jorge-andrade">Jorge Andrade</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789684321328" href="/book/show/8679390-vidas-paralelas">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114216150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer563675525530294377">n esta obra el intelectual romano describe la vida y obra de algunos de los personajes más importantes de la antigüedad en forma de pares (Alejandro y Julio Cesar, etc). Al final realiza un ejercicio de comparación entre ambos personajes biografiados.<br/>La cantidad de biografías escritas por Plutarco es desconocida, sin embargo y de acuerdo a lo planteado en la introducción de la edición leída, llegaron hasta nuestros días 50 biografías de entre las que se destacan: Rómulo, Pericles, el gran Ciceró</span>
  <span id="freeText563675525530294377" style="display:none">n esta obra el intelectual romano describe la vida y obra de algunos de los personajes más importantes de la antigüedad en forma de pares (Alejandro y Julio Cesar, etc). Al final realiza un ejercicio de comparación entre ambos personajes biografiados.<br />La cantidad de biografías escritas por Plutarco es desconocida, sin embargo y de acuerdo a lo planteado en la introducción de la edición leída, llegaron hasta nuestros días 50 biografías de entre las que se destacan: Rómulo, Pericles, el gran Cicerón, Fabio Máximo, Tiberio, Artajerjes II, Otón entre otros. La edición que cayó en mis manos y que corresponde a la colección "Sepan cuántos..." de Editorial Porrúa, contiene las siguientes pares de biografías:<br /><br /> * Teseo y Rómulo<br /> * Licurgo y Numa<br /> * Solón y Publícola<br /> * Pericles y Fabio Máximo<br /> * Alcibíades y Coriolano<br /> * Alejandro y Cayo Julio César<br /> * Demóstenes y Cicerón<br /> * Demetrio y Antonio<br /><br />Con respecto a la obra en sí, puedo decir que es netamente un obra biográfica y por lo tanto su intención única es la de informar y dejar un legado de conocimiento. No hay tratamiento acabado de la forma, cuya causa (no la única) la veo en el hecho de que es una traducción y por lo tanto se pierde la riqueza del lenguaje original.<br />Vidas Paralelas es una obra personal, escrita desde la subjetividad del autor y plasma en ella juicios y sentimientos propios de la época lo que nos permite e invita a involucrarnos en los hechos narrados.<br />No es de fácil lectura, a ratos se torna tediosa, pero esto es consecuencia de la intención del autor.<br />Es un libro recomendable para todos aquellos que deseen informarse o más que eso, conocer la opinión de un "contemporáneo" de los personajes biografiados o por lo menos alguien más cercano que nosotros.</span>

<div id="review_2744755500" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2744755500" />
    <a title="Zharel Anger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86221295-zharel-anger"><img alt="Zharel Anger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535924332p2/86221295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2744755500?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zharel Anger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zharel Anger" href="/user/show/86221295-zharel-anger">Zharel Anger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2744755500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4664516814924756861"> Douglas Adams, A.A. Milne, Shakespeare, and the Python Group were informed by these writings.<br/><br/> This is a great read into the morality of leadership and power in the ancient Greco-Roman world and is valuable research for those who want to write about the mindset of Greeks and Romans at the second century AD.<br/><br/> Plutarch’s writing is pleasant and easy, but the specifics of the histories in this work are incomplete. Indeed, he admits at the start that he is “writing biography, not history” and that “t</span>
  <span id="freeText4664516814924756861" style="display:none"> Douglas Adams, A.A. Milne, Shakespeare, and the Python Group were informed by these writings.<br /><br /> This is a great read into the morality of leadership and power in the ancient Greco-Roman world and is valuable research for those who want to write about the mindset of Greeks and Romans at the second century AD.<br /><br /> Plutarch’s writing is pleasant and easy, but the specifics of the histories in this work are incomplete. Indeed, he admits at the start that he is “writing biography, not history” and that “their great deeds (are) to be recorded by others.” What we gain is an insight into the quality the lives he writes about as well as what qualities were valued by Plutarch and why those qualities were valued.<br /><br /> The style of the narrative is entertaining as he besmirches other ‘historians’ for their lack of insight or their emphasis on irrelevant deeds over motivations and reason. The narrative style was adopted in part by Milne for the narrator in Once on a Time as well as by Douglas Adams for The Book in the Hitchhiker series. The influence is unmistakable.<br /><br /> The many strange anecdotes have also been employed by comedic writers and inform such works as Shakespeare and the Monty Python Group. I was pleasantly surprised to read Plutarch’s assessment of Greek currency which is replicated in Douglas Adam’s treatment of the Altairian dollar. Both currencies are cumbersome and a generally incompatible medium of exchange.<br /><br /> If you have the time to read this tome, you will gain greater insight and understanding into the stories of the countless authors who were influenced by this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4664516814924756861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2960375527" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2960375527" />
    <a title="Umar Farooq" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16562355-umar-farooq"><img alt="Umar Farooq" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481469152p2/16562355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2960375527?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Umar Farooq" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Umar Farooq" href="/user/show/16562355-umar-farooq">Umar Farooq</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2960375527" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5101101887147989318">A timeless classic. Plutarch has done a fantastic job capturing the personalities of so many well known roman and greek heroes/anti-heroes that I lost count at the end. Some of them I knew beforehand like Sulla or Pericles but most of them were new to me.<br/>The writing style is grand and covers a lot of side plots and names to the extent that it gets a bit hard to follow who is who after a while. Also the book covers semi-mythical men like Romulus also who are more like Arnold in terminator movies,</span>
  <span id="freeText5101101887147989318" style="display:none">A timeless classic. Plutarch has done a fantastic job capturing the personalities of so many well known roman and greek heroes/anti-heroes that I lost count at the end. Some of them I knew beforehand like Sulla or Pericles but most of them were new to me.<br />The writing style is grand and covers a lot of side plots and names to the extent that it gets a bit hard to follow who is who after a while. Also the book covers semi-mythical men like Romulus also who are more like Arnold in terminator movies, plowing through heaps of enemies with little resistance and no tact. So at times one has to hear the story with suspension of disbelief. It is strictly speaking not a history book in my opinion but a morality lesson for upcoming Roman generations by Plutarch.<br />The main characters are larger than life, ambitious and brilliant. Especially guys like Alcibiades and Caesar are just hell bent on being remembered as great. Even though Plutarch tries to attribute things mostly to gods, such men steal the thunder from gods at each step and bend the will of nature many a times. It is fascinating to see what a single human being can accomplish when pursuing glory. Believing in one's destiny is more important than destiny itself.<br />The mind blowing part is the comparison between two men at the end of two lives. Plutarch brings up attributes of the guys in a way I could never have thought about. One brilliant example is that of Coriolanus vs Alcibiades. I was surprised how a single event can be interpreted in so many ways.<br />Overall an amazing book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5101101887147989318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1808338136?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isaac" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isaac" href="/user/show/8967389-isaac">Isaac</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1808338136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12053331317000705264">I'd never heard of this book, but I learned from David McCullough that Truman's father gave him a copy as a child which he "poured over". I read a bit about it and found it was available as one huge audiobook so I figured I'd give it a chance. If nothing else, at least I can say I've read it!<br/><br/>Of the 50 "lives" there were maybe 15 (mostly Romans around Caesar) that I considered really great reads, and maybe another 10 that I was completely lost during (mostly Greeks after Themistocles), and the re</span>
  <span id="freeText12053331317000705264" style="display:none">I'd never heard of this book, but I learned from David McCullough that Truman's father gave him a copy as a child which he "poured over". I read a bit about it and found it was available as one huge audiobook so I figured I'd give it a chance. If nothing else, at least I can say I've read it!<br /><br />Of the 50 "lives" there were maybe 15 (mostly Romans around Caesar) that I considered really great reads, and maybe another 10 that I was completely lost during (mostly Greeks after Themistocles), and the rest somewhere in the middle. I think Plutarch assumed his readers would come with some understanding of Greek and Roman geography, social structure and history, not people like me who probably couldn't have found Greece on a map 2 months ago.<br /><br />Plutarch also digresses a lot which is occasionally really interesting or really funny, but often just confused me.<br /><br />Also while I read this I watched a lot of TV/Movies that were clearly based on the lives like HBO's Rome, Oliver Stone's (horrendous) Alexander, Shakespeare's Julius Caesar, Cleopatra, etc. All of which were made more enjoyable by the book and the book was made more enjoyable for the shows.<br /><br />It's been quite a journey, glad I muscled through. Someday I'll probably consume certain specific lives again, but I doubt anyone whose not a real serious historian needs to read this cover to cover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12053331317000705264" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_484952874" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484952874" />
    <a title="b bb bbbb bbbbbbbb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb"><img alt="b bb bbbb bbbbbbbb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576302401p2/15765304.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484952874?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="b bb bbbb bbbbbbbb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="b bb bbbb bbbbbbbb" href="/user/show/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb">b bb bbbb bbbbbbbb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780460004091" href="/book/show/279535.Lives_of_the_Noble_Greeks_and_Romans_2_Vols">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=antiquity">antiquity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484952874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3653686414971214296">As long as you've got a taste for epics this is great stuff. Its thirty six biographies of ancient greeks and romans as told by a slightly less ancient greek (Plutarch).<br/><br/> Usually a greek and a roman with similar aspects of character or life story will be paired together, have their individual stories told, and then be compared. <br/><br/>There is tons of social, political, scientific and philosophical context lurking among the lives. Whats most striking is how little government and social behavior has chan</span>
  <span id="freeText3653686414971214296" style="display:none">As long as you've got a taste for epics this is great stuff. Its thirty six biographies of ancient greeks and romans as told by a slightly less ancient greek (Plutarch).<br /><br /> Usually a greek and a roman with similar aspects of character or life story will be paired together, have their individual stories told, and then be compared. <br /><br />There is tons of social, political, scientific and philosophical context lurking among the lives. Whats most striking is how little government and social behavior has changed in the last two thousand years. <br /><br />Public opinion is pandered to and manipulated in exactly the same ways, politicians are still backstabbing and forming dubious alliances for their advancement. Lack of universal suffrage aside (women and slaves couldn't vote), the basic shape of democracy appears relatively similar then as now, though bloodier. Its humbling in a way - I'd expect things to have improved more in the last two thousand years.<br /><br />It took four months to read and was completely worth it. Its a little heavy on battles and war, but even those are made interesting by documenting the tricks and deceits, and using them to frame the behavior and character of the biography being told.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3653686414971214296" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('f1yHaXX7O1ELx60cd0zI7EyRYLyI9tof+1twxwELxaSbt3jUb/fH17r1hzlZiRSVQqbc9tEylx24yq9T0F9MgA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Out of this skirmish arose a stirring tale of war, which was recorded in the oldest extant epic poem in French. The Song of Roland, written by an unknown poet, tells of Charlemagne’s warrior nephew, Lord of the Breton Marches, who valiantly leads his men into battle against the Saracens, but dies in the massacre, defiant to the end. In majestic verses, the battle becomes a symbolic struggle between Christianity and Islam, while Roland’s last stand is the ultimate expression of honour and feudal values of twelfth-century But Chaucer\'s genius needs no historical introduction; it bursts forth from every page of The Canterbury Tales.\n\nIf we trust the General Prologue, Chaucer intended that each pilgrim should tell two tales on the way to Canterbury and two tales on the way back. He never finished his enormous project and even the completed tales were not finally revised. Scholars are uncertain about the order of the tales. He demonstrates the critical role of maritime trade to the civilizations of ancient Egypt, Mesopotamia, and the Indus Valley. He reacquaints us with the great seafaring cultures of antiquity like those of the Phoenicians and Greeks, as well as those of India and Southeast and East Asia, who parlayed their navigational skills, shipbuilding techniques, and commercial acumen to establish thriving overseas colonies and trade routes in the centuries leading up to the age of European expansion. And finally, his narrative traces how commercial shipping and naval warfare brought about the enormous demographic, cultural, and political changes that have globalized the world throughout the post–Cold War era.  This tremendously readable intellectual adventure shows us the world in a new light, in which the sea reigns supreme. We find out how a once-enslaved East African king brought Islam to his people, what the American “sail-around territories” were, and what the Song Dynasty did with twenty-wheel, human-powered paddleboats with twenty paddle wheels and up to three hundred crew. Above all, Paine makes clear how the rise and fall of civilizations can be linked to the sea. Lovecraft<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.22 avg rating &mdash; 37,117 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1926\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4991129386020695997\">This collection spans Lovecraft’s literary career, and charts the development of his ‘cosmicist’ philosophy; the belief that behind the veil of our blinkered everyday lives lies another reality, too terrible for the human mind to comprehend. In stori<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4991129386020695997\" style=\"display:none\">This collection spans Lovecraft’s literary career, and charts the development of his ‘cosmicist’ philosophy; the belief that behind the veil of our blinkered everyday lives lies another reality, too terrible for the human mind to comprehend. In stories written in the gothic tradition, narrators recount their descent into madness and despair. Through their investigations into the unexplained, they tug at the thin threads that separate our world from another of indescribable horror. ‘“ Great God! I never dreamed of THIS!”’ screams occultist Harley Warren in ‘The Statement of Randolph Carter’, as he begs his companion to bury him alive. Another early piece, ‘The Outsider’ – a tragic and emotive evocation of loneliness and desolation – follows a man’s escape from his castle in a desperate search for human contact, but the loathsome truth he discovers destroys his mind.\n\nIn later tales, such as the iconic ‘The Call of Cthulhu’ and ‘The Whisperer in Darkness’, Lovecraft reaches into the cosmos, bridging the divide between horror and science fiction. The extra-terrestrial ‘gods’ and cursed histories that would emerge from these stories now form the cornerstones of Lovecraft’s unique mythology: the Cthulhu Mythos. This fictional universe, built in large part by his friend and most ardent supporter August Derleth, has in the years since been reimagined in myriad forms, and continues to act as a haunted playground for countless illustrators, fans and authors.\n\nThis edition, based on its sister limited edition, marries Lovecraft’s best-known fiction with two modern masters of the macabre, the acclaimed artist Dan Hillier and author Alan Moore. In his beautifully crafted new preface, Moore finds Lovecraft at once at odds with and integral to the time in which he lived: ‘the improbable embodiment of an estranged world in transition’. Yet, despite his prejudices and parochialisms, he ‘possessed a voice and a perspective both unique in modern literature’.\n\nHillier’s six mesmerising, portal-like illustrations embrace the alien realities that lurk among the gambrel roofs of Lovecraft’s landscapes. By splicing Victorian portraits and lithographs with cosmic and Lovecraftian symbolism, each piece – like the stories themselves – pulls apart the familiar to reveal what lies beneath.\n\nThe edition itself shimmers with Lovecraft’s ‘unknown colours’, bound in purple and greens akin to both the ocean depths and mysteries from outer space. The cover is embossed with a mystical design by Hillier, while a monstrous eye stares blankly from the slipcase.\n\nContent:\n1. Dagon\n2. The Statement of Randolph Carter\n3. The Rats in the Walls\n11. The Festival\n12. He\n13. Cool Air\n14. The Call of Cthulhu\n15. The Colour Out of Space\n16. The Whisperer in Darkness\n17. The Shadow Over Innsmouth\n18.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/31015.Plutarch">About Plutarch</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/31015.Plutarch"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1467731032p3/31015.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/31015.Plutarch">Plutarch
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
520
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14776042596709490132">Plutarch, later named, upon becoming a Roman citizen, Lucius Mestrius Plutarchus; (AD 46 – AD 120) was a Greek historian, biographer, and essayist, known primarily for his Parallel Lives and Moralia. He is classified as a Middle Platonist. Plutarch's surviving works were written in Greek, but intended for both Greek and Roman readers.<br/><br/><br/></span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
